Dream of the Blind Jurist: Prophet Endorses Ash'ari Stance on al-Harf
A blind jurist dreams of the Prophet and is told to say as the Ash'aris say regarding al-harf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a prophetic dream narrated in the Sunni tradition was used to endorse the Ash'ari position on whether the letter/sound (al-harf) is created or uncreated.

Core Idea
A prophetic dream commands: Say as the Ash'aris say about al-harf.

In this passage, we encounter a remarkable dream narrative from the Sunni tradition. A blind jurist, after a quarrel with his father, falls asleep in a mosque and dreams of the Prophet. The dream becomes a vehicle for resolving a contentious theological dispute: whether the 'letter' or 'sound' of the Quran is created or uncreated. The Prophet's triple command to 'say as the Ash'aris said' serves as a powerful endorsement of the Ash'ari school on this matter.

Guided Path
1
Context of the dream
Read the opening of the narrative to understand the spur dynamics: the jurist's anger with his father leads him to the mosque, where he falls asleep and dreams of the Prophet.
Notice the Prophet's initial anger and the jurist's appeal to mercy.
2
The Prophet's confirmation of the hadith questions
The jurist asks about two hadith-related issues. Observe how the Prophet confirms them, establishing the dream's credibility.
These details reinforce that the dream is a genuine prophetic visitation.
3
The central question about al-harf
The jurist presents the dispute: some say al-harf is created, others uncreated. Focus on the Prophet's response and its triple repetition.
The triple command emphasizes the importance of following the Ash'ari position.
4
The aftermath and change of opinion
After waking, the jurist's view of al-Ghazali's al-Risala al-Qudsiyya improves, linking the dream to Ash'ari scholarship.
This shows the practical impact of the dream on the jurist's attitude.
5
Takeaways
Reflect on the theological significance: the use of dreams as a source of guidance in Sunni Islam and the endorsement of Ash'ari theology.
Consider how this narrative reinforces the Ash'ari stance on the Quran's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدثني الشيخ أبو عبدالله طرخان بن ماضي بن جوشن المقرى الفقيه الضرير، قال جرى بينى وبين والدي كلام غضبت منه فخرجت إلى مسجد السوسي بالشاغور ونمت فيه نهاراً ، فبينما أنا نائم إذ رأيت في المنام كأن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قد دخل من باب الشباك الذي من شرقي المسجد، فجلست وقلت السلام عليك يارسول الله فكان كالمغضب على فقال لى أنت تقرأ القرآن وتغضب أباك، فقلت الآن أرجو أن يغفر الله لى ماكان مني في حق أبى بحضورك فإن الله عز وجل قال (وما أرسلناك إلا . رحمة للعالمين) فكأنه رضى عنى ودعا لى وأخذ ليقوم فسألته عن أبي حميد الساعدى فى سؤاله إياه عن كيفية الصلاة عليه صلى وسلم فقال صدق أبو حميد وأثنى عليه، وسألته عن قوله لعلى بن أبى الله عنه « لاتبرز فخذك ولا تنظر إلى فخذ جي ولاميت، فقال صدق أنا أمرته بذلك ثم خرج من المسجد فاتبعته وقلت يا رسول الله إن قوما يقولون إن الحرف مخلوق وقوما يقولون غير مخلوق وقد تحيرنا بينهم فما ندرى مانقول فقال ( قل كما قالت الأشعرية ) فقلت يارسول الله كذا كما قالت الأشعرية على وجه الاستنكار فقال ثلاث مرات ( قل كما قالت الأشعرية ) ثم توجه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نحـو قـبـلة الشاغور خارجا من الباب وانا اقول هذا المزمل هذا المدثر، وهو واضع يديه على صدره کهيئة المصلى فوضعت يدي اليسرى على يده وانا اقول هذا المزمل هذا المدثر، ثم استيقظت وكانت عندى الرسالة القدسية للغزالي وكنت لا احسن رأيي فيها واقول ما اصنع بها فحسن رأيي فيها بعد ذلك وقرأتها وقرأت غيرها والحمد الله،
The shaykh Abu 'Abd Allah Tarkhan ibn Madi ibn Jawshan al-Muqri' al-Faqih al-Darir narrated to me, saying: There occurred between me and my father some words at which I became angry, so I went out to the mosque of al-Susi in al-Shaghur and slept there during the day. While I was asleep, I saw in a dream as if the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, entered through the window door on the eastern side of the mosque. I sat up and said, 'Peace be upon you, O Messenger of Allah.' He seemed angry with me and said, 'You recite the Quran and anger your father?' I said, 'Now I hope that Allah will forgive me for what was from me regarding my father in your presence, for Allah Almighty said: (And We have not sent you except as a mercy to the worlds).' He seemed pleased with me and prayed for me, and he began to stand. I asked him about Abu Hamid al-Sa'idi regarding his question to him about how to invoke blessings upon him, peace be upon him, and he said, 'Abu Hamid spoke truth,' and he praised him. I asked him about his saying to 'Ali ibn Abi Talib: 'Do not expose your thigh, and do not look at the thigh of a living or dead person,' and he said, 'He spoke truth; I commanded him that.' Then he left the mosque, and I followed him, saying, 'O Messenger of Allah, some people say that al-harf (the letter/sound) is created, and some say it is uncreated, and we are confused between them; we do not know what to say.' He said, 'Say as the Ash'aris said.' I said, 'O Messenger of Allah, like that, as the Ash'aris said?' in a tone of disapproval, but he said three times, 'Say as the Ash'aris said.' Then the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, turned towards the qibla of al-Shaghur, exiting through the door, and I was saying, 'This is the wrapped one, this is the enveloped one,' while he had placed his hands on his chest like one praying. I placed my left hand on his hand, saying, 'This is the wrapped one, this is the enveloped one.' Then I woke up, and I had with me al-Risala al-Qudsiyya of al-Ghazali, and I had not a good opinion of it, and I used to say, 'What should I do with it?' But after that, my opinion of it improved, and I read it and read other things, and praise be to Allah.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a dream narrative that serves to validate the Ash'ari position on a disputed theological issue—whether the letter/sound (al-harf) is created or uncreated. The protagonist, a blind jurist, dreams of the Prophet after a quarrel with his father. The Prophet initially appears angry but becomes pleased after the jurist invokes the verse about mercy. The jurist then asks about two hadith-related matters—the proper form of blessing on the Prophet and a prohibition regarding thighs—and the Prophet confirms them. Finally, the jurist asks about the dispute over al-harf. The Prophet explicitly commands him three times to 'say as the Ash'aris said.' This triple affirmation strongly endorses the Ash'ari belief. The dream also leads the jurist to change his previously negative opinion of al-Ghazali's work, indicating that the Ash'ari position aligns with Ghazali's theology.

مخلوق
غير مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophetic dreams cannot be used to settle theological disputes.
Correction: In the Sunni tradition, prophetic dreams are considered valid indications of truth, especially when they align with the Sharia and are reported by reliable individuals.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet commanded the blind jurist to 'say as the Ash'aris said' about al-harf. Have you ever submitted to a theological position simply because it came from a reliable source, even when your own heart was uncertain? Will you now humbly accept guidance, even if it requires setting aside your personal opinion?
?
The jurist had a low opinion of al-Ghazali's al-Risala al-Qudsiyya until the dream changed his heart. Is there a book, a scholar, or a teaching you have dismissed without truly examining it? Will you open your mind to reconsider it, seeking the truth wherever it may be?
?
The Prophet was angry at the jurist for angering his father. How does your treatment of your parents affect your relationship with Allah? Are you willing to seek forgiveness from them and from Allah, and to change your behavior starting today?

The Prophet's Dream Command: Follow the Ash'ariyya
Narrative of a dream where the Prophet instructs to adopt the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a prophetic dream affirmed the Ash'ari theological position and the significance of such dreams in Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
The Prophet's dream command validates the Ash'ari position as correct.

This lesson presents a remarkable narrative from Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari, where an unidentified person dreams of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and receives a direct command to adopt the position of the Ash'ariyya. The dreamer initially hesitates, but the Prophet insists three times, then turns toward the qibla. Upon waking, the dreamer finds new appreciation for al-Ghazali's al-Risala al-Qudsiyya, linking the dream to a broader theological affirmation. This story serves as a powerful testament to the Ash'ari school's legitimacy within Sunni Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream narrative
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the dreamer's initial confusion and the Prophet's repeated command.
The command 'say as the Ash'ariyya said' is given three times for emphasis.
2
Identify the dreamer's reaction
Observe how the dreamer responds with surprise and disapproval, and how the Prophet insists. This shows the dream is not immediately accepted but is authoritative.
3
Reflect on the dream's aftermath
Consider how the dream changes the dreamer's view of al-Ghazali's work. This connects the dream to practical scholarly engagement.
4
Discuss the doctrinal implication
Understand that the dream is not about school superiority but about affirming the correctness of the Ash'ari position, as a guide for the perplexed.

This passage narrates a dream in which the Prophet commands the dreamer to adopt the opinion of the Ash'ariyya. The dreamer initially expresses doubt, but the Prophet insists three times. Upon waking, the dreamer's attitude toward al-Ghazali's al-Risala al-Qudsiyya improves, indicating a positive effect of the dream.

Dream of al-Ash'ari Affirming His Madhhab
Narrative of a dream where al-Ash'ari declares his madhhab true three times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream narrative was used to affirm the truth of the Ash'ari madhhab.

Core Idea
A dream of al-Ash'ari himself confirms his madhhab as true.

This passage reports a dream vision in which the founder of the Ash'ari school, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, appears and explicitly declares three times that his madhhab is true. Such narratives carry weight in traditional Islamic scholarship as signs of divine approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the dream narrative carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the triple repetition of 'مذهب الأشعرى حق'.
The dream is presented with a chain of transmission (isnad).
2
Identify the participants
The narrator relates a dream seen by Abu al-Qasim al-Zubayr, who tells it to the narrator after being asked.
Abu al-Hasan refers to Imam al-Ash'ari.
3
Reflect on the dream's setting
The dream takes place in the Umayyad Mosque in Damascus, near Bab al-Barrada. This sacred location adds weight.
4
Understand the confirmation
The dreamer's companion affirms the dream's truthfulness, arguing that al-Ash'ari is truthful and now in paradise ('Dar al-Haqq').
This is a theological argument for accepting the dream as valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكى لي بعض اصحابنا عن أبي القسم بن ابراهيم بن حسين الدقاق المعروف بالزبير رؤيا رآها فلقيته في الجامع بدمشق فسألته عن رؤياه وقلت له : بلغنى أنك رأيت الفقيه أبا الحسن رحمه في المنام فقال أى والذى قبض روحه لقد رأيته فى المنام كأنه ههنا وأشار إلى مكان من الجامع بقرب باب البرادة وخلفته وهو داخل إلى صدر المسجد فقال لي يا أبا القسم مذهب الأشعرى حق مذهب الأشعري حق مذهب الأشعرى حق ثم استيقظت فقلت له ما قال لك حق فانه كان صادق اللهجة وهو في دار حق فلا يقول إلا الحق
And one of our companions narrated to me from Abu al-Qasim ibn Ibrahim ibn Husayn al-Daqqaq known as al-Zubayr, a dream he saw. I met him in the mosque in Damascus and asked him about his dream. I said to him: 'It reached me that you saw the faqih Abu al-Hasan (may Allah have mercy on him) in the dream.' He said: 'Yes, by the One who took his soul, I saw him as if he was here,' and he pointed to a spot near Bab al-Barrada, and I left him as he was entering the courtyard of the mosque. He said to me: 'O Abu al-Qasim, the madhhab of al-Ash'ari is true, the madhhab of al-Ash'ari is true, the madhhab of al-Ash'ari is true.' Then I woke up. I said to him: 'What he said to you is true, for he was truthful in speech, and he is in the Abode of Truth, so he only speaks the truth.'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage reports a dream where al-Ash'ari himself appears and affirms three times that his madhhab is true. The dreamer interprets this as authentic because the deceased scholar is truthful and resides in the Abode of Truth (paradise). This serves as a divine confirmation of the Ash'ari school.

Key Terms
مذهب الأشعرى
حق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dream means al-Ash'ari's madhhab is the only true one.
Correction: The dream affirms that the Ash'ari madhhab is true, not that it is exclusively true. Other madhahib can also be true within Sunni Islam.

Checkpoint

A) Once

C) Three times
D) Four times

Key Takeaway
The dream vision serves as a powerful testimony to the truth of the Ash'ari madhhab, affirmed by the founder himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This dream was transmitted through a chain of narrators. How much of your own belief is built on transmitted reports versus personal conviction? Are you willing to submit to truth even if it comes to you through a single trustworthy narrator? What would it cost you?

Al-Qushayri's Poem: Love of Abu Bakr and the Ash'ari Madhhab
Poem by al-Qushayri linking love of Abu Bakr to the Ash'ari madhhab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Qushayri affirms his allegiance to both the first caliph Abu Bakr and the Ash'ari school, and why he dismisses criticism of these commitments.

Core Idea
Love of Abu Bakr and the Ash'ari madhhab are two inseparable principles for al-Qushayri.

In this short but powerful poem, the renowned scholar al-Qushayri declares two foundational commitments that define his identity: love for Abu Bakr al-Siddiq and adherence to the Ash'ari theological school. The poem serves as both a personal testimony and a dismissal of detractors. As you read, consider how al-Qushayri links these two seemingly separate stances into a unified declaration of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and its translation

The poem is only two lines; savor its brevity.
2
Identify the two things
The poet says 'Two things' – identify what they are and why he considers criticism of them evidence that the critic is not truly with him.
The two things are 'love of Abu Bakr' and 'belief in the Ash'ari madhhab'.
3
Reflect on the historical context

Love and Creed as True Provision

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the poet declares love of the Prophet and adherence to Ash'ari creed as the only provision that benefits a person on the Day of Resurrection.

Core Idea
Love of the Prophet and Ash'ari belief are the ultimate spiritual provision.

This brief poem distills the essence of the poet's conviction: on the Day of Resurrection, material goods are worthless. Only two things matter—love for the Prophet and sound creed as taught by Imam al-Ash'ari. The lines are a personal testament but also a teaching for all believers.

Guided Path

The poem is a single sentence with a clear premise and conclusion.

The word 'ثم' (then) may imply sequence or simply addition.

This connects to the common misunderstanding that provision means material goods.

The Three Pillars of Certain Truth: Ash'ari, Shafi'i, and Ibn al-'Ala'
Poem enumerating three conditions for certain truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three components of orthodox truth as presented in this poem: Ash'ari theology, Shafi'i fiqh, and the grammatical school of Ibn al-'Ala'.

Core Idea
True orthodoxy unites right theology, law, and language.

This poem, transmitted through a chain of scholars, succinctly states that being upon certain truth requires agreement in three areas: theology (usul al-din), jurisprudence (fiqh), and grammar (nahw). The poet presents these as the complete package of Islamic orthodoxy, warning against deviation in any of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem with translation
Read the Arabic poem slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the three conditions listed.
The poem is a single line of verse.
2
Identify the three conditions
Find the three statements: 'قول الأشعرى المسدد', 'قول الامام الشافعي المؤيد', and 'حرف ابن العلاء'. Note the rejection of 'رأى المبرد'.
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why the poet combines theology, law, and grammar. This reflects the idea that complete orthodoxy encompasses creed, practice, and language.
The poem is not just about personal piety but about scholarly tradition.
4
Ask: Why exclude al-Mubarrad?
Consider the historical rivalry between the grammatical schools of Basra and Kufa. Ibn al-'Ala' represents the Basran school, while al-Mubarrad is a key Kufan figure.
This shows the poet's preference for the Basran school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنشدنى الشيخ الزاهد أبو محمد عبدالوارث بن عبدالغني الأصولي لبعضهم وكتب إلى الشيخ أبو القسم نصر بن نصر العكبري يخبرني عن القاضي أبى المعالى عزيزى بن عبد الملك قال أنشدنا القاضي الإمام أبو الحسن هبة الله بن عبد الله السيبي مدرس وملقن [؟] ولى العهد في العالمين أبى عبدالله بن محمد بن الامام امير المؤمنين القائم بأمر الله عبد الله أبى القسم عبد الله -جعفر إذا كنت في علم الأصول موافقا بعقدك قول الأشعرى المسدد وعاملت مولاك الكريم مخالصا بقول الامام الشافعي المؤيد وأتقنت حرف ابن العلاء مجردا ولم تعد في الإعراب رأى المبرد فأنت على الحق اليقين موافق شريعة خير المرسلين محمد
The ascetic shaykh Abu Muhammad 'Abd al-Warith ibn 'Abd al-Ghani al-Usuli recited to me for some of them, and wrote to me from the shaykh Abu al-Qasim Nasr ibn Nasr al-'Ukbari informing me from the qadi Abu al-Ma'ali 'Azizi ibn 'Abd al-Malik who said: The qadi imam Abu al-Hasan Hibat Allah ibn 'Abd Allah al-Saybi, teacher and tutor [؟] of the crown prince of the worlds, Abu 'Abd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn the Imam Commander of the Faithful al-Qa'im bi-Amr Allah 'Abd Allah Abu al-Qasim 'Abd Allah - Ja'far, recited to us: If you are in the science of principles (usul) agreeing with your belief in the well-guided statement of al-Ash'ari, and you treat your Generous Lord sincerely with the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i the supported, and you master the letter of Ibn al-'Ala' purely and do not adopt the opinion of al-Mubarrad in grammar, then you are upon certain truth, agreeing with the Sharia of the best of messengers, Muhammad.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This long poem sets out three conditions for being upon certain truth: first, accepting the Ash'ari statement in usul al-din (theology); second, following Imam al-Shafi'i in jurisprudence with sincerity; third, mastering the grammatical school of Ibn al-'Ala' to the exclusion of al-Mubarrad. The poem thus combines theological, legal, and linguistic orthodoxy.

Key Terms
علم الأصول
قول الأشعرى
قول الامام الشافعي
ابن العلاء
المبرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem means one must follow these exact schools to be saved.
Correction: The poem is not about salvation but about scholarly orthodoxy; it presents these schools as representing a complete and correct tradition.

Checkpoint

D) Ash'ari theology, Maliki fiqh, and the grammar of al-Mubarrad

Key Takeaway
The poem teaches that true orthodoxy requires correct belief (Ash'ari), correct practice (Shafi'i), and correct language (Basran grammar).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The poem links theology, law, and language as necessary for certain truth. Have you been neglecting one of these areas in your pursuit of knowledge? How might that imbalance affect your certainty and your relationship with Allah?

The Call to the Ash'ari Creed
Poem warning against desire and urging Ash'ari creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poem's exhortation to adhere to the Ash'ari creed as the cure for blind desire.

Core Idea
Following desire blinds the heart; truth is found in the Ash'ari creed.

This poem, recited to Ibn 'Asakir, starkly contrasts the confusion of those who follow their whims with the clarity of the Ash'ari 'aqidah. Despite textual uncertainties in its middle lines, the final exhortation rings clear: hold fast to the truth and believe in the creed of al-Ash'ari. The poem serves as a powerful reminder that theological guidance requires submission to sound doctrine, not personal desire.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic poem once, noting the bracketed uncertain parts, then read the translation. Focus on the clear final line.
The body is OCR-corrupted but the ending is intact.
2
Identify the problem described
Notice the poet's description of people: blind confusion, following desire, turning from guidance, inventing lies.
3
Find the poet's solution
The poet's solution is stated in the last line: stick to the truth and believe in the creed of al-Ash'ari.
4
Reflect on the role of desire
Consider why desire is described as blinding. How does the Ash'ari creed provide clarity?
Desire leads to subjective whims; the creed is objective truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنشدني الشيخ ابو الفتح ناصر بن عبد الرحمن القرشي لبعضهم : أصبح الناس في عمى بين جعلوا دينـهـ[؟] اه وممترى هوى والهوى غير مبصـ[؟] وتعاموا عن الهدى ليس فيهم بمنكر وهو من جهلهم برى بهوا[؟] الله بالورى حرم الرشد من غدا يتعامى ويفـ[؟]ـترى ستری فالحق لا تزغ واعتقد عقد الأشعرى

Commentary
This poem criticizes people who follow their desires and ignore guidance. The poet declares that those who are blind and invent lies are deprived of right guidance, and urges the reader to adhere to the truth by believing in the Ash'ari creed. Are you aware of any area in your life where your desires have blinded you to the truth, even after hearing this poem's warning? What specific step will you take today to expose that area to the light of sound 'aqīdah?
?
If the poet says 'cling to the truth and do not deviate, and believe in the creed of al-Ash'ari', what is the one belief or action you have been justifying as 'just a personal opinion' that actually contradicts this creed? Will you go and check your beliefs against a trusted Ash'ari 'aqidah text this week?
?
The poem describes people as 'not among them is one who denounces (evil)'. When you see someone close to you following desire in matters of creed, do you stay silent? What would it cost you to speak the truth in love, as this poem commands?

Poetic Refutation of the Created Qur'an
Poem praising al-Ash'ari and refuting created speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a classical poem defends al-Ash'ari and rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that God's speech is created.

Core Idea
The poem declares that God's speech is not like created speech.

This poem serves as both praise for al-Ash'ari and a sharp theological rebuttal. It laments the fragmentation of the early community and condemns those who, following desire, corrupted doctrine. The key line targets the Mu'tazilite assertion that the Qur'an is created, affirming instead that God's speech is exalted above being like heard speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem's opening
Notice the poet's direct address: 'Take what appears to you or leave; the sayings of innovation have multiplied.' This sets a confrontational tone.
The poem is a response to theological deviation.
2
Identify the praise of al-Ash'ari
The poet asks for Allah's aid for al-Ash'ari, describing him as an Imam who benefited creation and spread guidance.
Al-Ash'ari is presented as the defender of orthodoxy.
3
Find the central doctrinal point
Look for the line about attributing to Allah speech 'like heard speech'. The poet rejects this as an innovation.
This is the core refutation of the created Qur'an.
4
Understand the poet's disavowal
The poet declares he disowns those who hold this view, calling their actions ugly abominations.
Strong language shows the seriousness of the error.

Source Text

أنشدني ابو محمد عبد الله بن محمد الاسكندراني لأبي القسم الجزري الاسكندراني :
خذ ما بدا لك أو فدع كثرت مقالات البدع
ان النبي المصـ[؟] ورضی به لعيـ[؟] طفي[؟]

ـنا[؟] يخ[؟] الديانة والورع

Abu Muhammad Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Iskandarani recited to me for Abu al-Qasim al-Jazari al-Iskandarani:
Take what appears to you or leave; the sayings of innovation have multiplied.
Indeed, the Prophet [؟] and with him [؟] pleased? a religion of [؟] so come [؟] law [؟].
Lord Most High is exalted.
Religion was one until what was united dispersed.
A people led astray by desire, and others followed it [؟] poison.
May God aid [؟] al-Ash'ari, the Imam of [؟] and through him creation has benefited.
[؟] of piety and scrupulousness.
He spread the discourse with guidance and cut their terrible arguments.
Until [؟] light is sought from his light, and God perfects what He makes.
Whoever said [؟] his article has erred in the path and introduced innovation.
None denies his speech except a foolish, vile brother.
The people of intellect awake, for dawn has split the horizon.
They attributed to the Lord Most High what His saying from Him? They claimed that the speech of his Lord Most High is exalted, like heard speech.

Commentary
This passage is a poetic panegyric and polemic. The poem begins by addressing the reader to accept or leave the content, as innovations have multiplied. It laments the fragmentation of original unity in religion, blaming groups led by desire and imitation. The poet then praises Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari as an Imam who benefited creation and defended the faith. He describes how al-Ash'ari spread guidance and refuted opponents' arguments, so that people seek light from his teachings. The central doctrinal point comes when the poet criticizes those who ascribe to God the claim that His speech is like heard (i.e., created) speech. This is a clear rejection of the Mu'tazilite view that the Quran is created. The poet disavows such people for committing ugly innovations.

Key Takeaway
The poem establishes that affirming the Qur'an as uncreated is a defining mark of Sunni orthodoxy, and that al-Ash'ari is the champion of this belief.

?

?
The poet laments that 'religion was one until what was united dispersed.' Have you ever contributed to the fragmentation of unity in your own community by promoting or silently accepting innovations in aqeedah? What is one step you can take to restore unity on foundational beliefs?

A Poem in Praise of Al-Ash'ari's Defense of Sunnah
Poem praising Al-Ash'ari as defender of Sunnah and refuter of innovators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how this poem celebrates Imam al-Ash'ari as a defender of the Sunnah and a refuter of innovators, and appreciate his scholarly contributions as highlighted in the poem.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari is praised as the enemy of innovators and the one who gathered the guidance of the Muslims.

This poem is a heartfelt eulogy for Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, praising his role in defending the Sunnah and refuting innovators. The poet highlights al-Ash'ari's piety, his reliance on the Prophet's teachings, and his prolific scholarly output. As you read the poem, notice how the poet uses vivid imagery to convey the impact of al-Ash'ari's work.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation, allowing the poet's voice to come through. Note the opening address and the final prayer.
The poem is structured with rhyming couplets, typical of classical Arabic praise poetry.
2
Identify the main praises of al-Ash'ari
The poem describes al-Ash'ari as 'enemy of innovators', 'defender of traditions', and 'one who set proofs for Sunnis'. List these attributes.
3
Notice the mention of his works
The poem specifically mentions al-Ibana and al-Luma' as sufficient, but also says he wrote over 200 works. This shows his vast contribution.
Al-Ibana and al-Luma' are among his most famous works.
4
Reflect on the impact described
The poet says his works are recited on pulpits and feared by non-Muslims. Think about why such praise is given to a theologian.
This reflects the high esteem al-Ash'ari holds in Sunni tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاهجر دمشق وأهلها واسكن بصرى أو زرع هناك يمكن أن يصدق ما تقول ويستمع واعلم بأن الأشعري عدو أصحاب البدع فهو المجيد الذب عن سنن الرسول وما شرع [؟] رجل تقي عالم [؟] جمع الديانة والورع رفع الآلهة [؟] محله عند البرية فارتفع واختار ما قال الرسول من الأصول وما اخترع لكنه نصب الدليل لمن تسنن واتبع وأبان أن العقل من آية أو سنة [؟] لا ينفي الصواب المتبع كان الرسول بها صدع يا حسن ما أبدى لنا وجه الدليل وما انتزع فغدا به شمل الهدى للمسلمين قد اجتمع وتفرقت فرق الضلال وذل مذموم الشيع وتعطلت ممن يعطل بعد كثرتهم [؟] قصد الجدال فما قمع فلأي حزب منهم أمه ذو بدعة لحجاجه إلا انقطع لو لم يصنف عمره غير الإبانة واللمع لكفى فكيف وقد تفتن في العلوم بما جمع جموعة تربي على المائتين مما قد صنع لم يأل في تصنيفها أخذا بأحسن ما استمع فهدى بها المسترشدين ومن تصفحها انتفع تتلى معاني كتبه فوق المنابر في الجمع ويخاف من إفحامه أهل الكنائس والبيع فهو الشجا في حلق من ترك المحجة وابتدع فعليه رحمة ربه ما غاب نجم أو طلع
So leave Damascus and its people, and settle in Busra or Zar', there perhaps they will believe what you say and listen. And know that al-Ash'ari is the enemy of the people of innovation, for he is the one who excellently defends the traditions of the Messenger and what he legislated [?] a pious scholar [?] who combined piety and scrupulousness; he raised the status of divinity [?] at his place among creation, so he rose. And he chose what the Messenger said of principles and did not invent. But he set up proofs for those who follow the Sunnah and follow. And he made clear that the intellect from a verse or a sunna does not negate the correct following, for the Messenger proclaimed by it. O how beautiful what he showed us of the face of evidence and what he extracted! So through him the comprehensive guidance of Muslims gathered, and the sects of misguidance scattered and the blameworthy parties were humiliated, and those who abandon (the truth) became inactive after their abundance [?] the intention of argumentation fell and was not suppressed. For any party among them that has a people of innovation, his argument is not cut off. If he had not authored in his life except al-Ibana and al-Luma', it would have sufficed. How (then) when he excelled in the sciences with what he collected: a collection that numbers over two hundred, from what he produced? He did not fail in authoring them, taking the best of what he heard. So he guided by them the seekers of guidance, and whoever examines them benefits. The meanings of his books are recited on the pulpits in the congregations, and the people of churches and synagogues fear his refutation. So he is a thorn in the throat of whoever leaves the path and innovates. So upon him be the mercy of his Lord as long as a star rises or sets.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a poetic eulogy for Abul Hasan al-Ash'ari. The poet begins by addressing someone to leave Damascus and go to Busra or Zar' where he might be believed. Then he declares that al-Ash'ari is the enemy of innovators and the defender of the Sunnah. He describes al-Ash'ari as a pious scholar who raised the status of divinity [?] and chose only what the Prophet legislated, not inventing anything. He set up proofs for those who follow the Sunnah. He made clear that reason does not negate the correct following of revelation, because the Prophet himself proclaimed by it. The poet marvels at the beauty of al-Ash'ari's evidence. Through him, the guidance of Muslims was gathered, the sects of misguidance were scattered, and those who abandoned the truth were left inactive. He says that even if al-Ash'ari had only authored al-Ibana and al-Luma, that would have sufficed; but he composed over two hundred works, taking the best of what he heard. His books guide seekers of guidance, and his meanings are recited on pulpits. The people of churches and synagogues fear his refutation. He is a thorn in the throat of those who leave the straight path and innovate. The poem ends with a prayer for mercy on him.

الأشعري
الإبانة
اللمع
البدعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ash'ari's works are few and limited in impact.
Correction: The poem notes that he authored over 200 works, and even just two of them (al-Ibana and al-Luma') would have sufficed to guide. His works are recited on pulpits and feared by non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Ash'ari is celebrated in this poem as a tireless defender of the Sunnah, whose over 200 works, especially al-Ibana and al-Luma', guided Muslims and silenced innovators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Ash'ari's works are recited from pulpits and feared by non-Muslims. How often do you engage with the works of the scholars who defended the Sunnah? Will you read at least one of al-Ash'ari's books this month?
?
The poem says al-Ash'ari 'combined piety and scrupulousness'. In your pursuit of knowledge, do you prioritize piety as much as intellectual understanding? What step will you take to increase your taqwa alongside your learning?

The Caliphs' Support and the People's Confusion about Divine Decree
Ibn al-Khall praises caliphs and notes people's confusion about decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Khall's poem connects the support of Abbasid caliphs to the confusion among people regarding divine decree.

Core Idea
The caliphs supported the truth, but people fell into confusion about God's decree.

This poem, delivered by Ibn al-Khall, begins by praising two Abbasid caliphs—al-Mu'tadid and al-Muktafi—for their role in upholding justice. It then describes how people became enraged and fell into disagreement about a matter that became well-known: the attribution of fate to God. This sets the stage for the later theological clarification by al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem excerpt
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the mention of caliphs and the people's reaction.
The poem is recited in praise of al-Isfarayini but focuses on al-Ash'ari's role.
2
Identify the caliphs
Find the names al-Mu'tadid and al-Muktafi in the passage. Understand their association with supporting the truth.
Al-Mu'tadid is described as a supporter of the oppressed, al-Muktafi as one who presents al-Muqtadir.
3
Analyze the people's confusion
The poem says people became enraged and differed about something that became well-known. The phrase 'هو الله ومن لم يقل ذاك أحال القدرا' indicates that some denied that God decrees or attribute fate to God incorrectly.
The exact meaning is obscured by OCR, but it likely refers to the issue of divine decree.
4
Reflect on the historical context
Consider that this poem was written during a time of theological disputes, and the caliphs' support was crucial for the clarification of truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انشدنا الشيخ أبو الحسين بن المبارك بن محمد البغدادي المعروف بابن الخل ببغداد في المدرسة النظامية قصيدة لنفسه مدح بها الشيخ ابا الفتوح محمد بن الفضل بن محمد الاسفرائنی رحمة الله عليه وذكر منها قوله: أزري [؟] منهم من المعتضد الناس فلم يك للمظلوم إلا وزرا وتلاه المكتفى بالله عن كل شيء يقدم المقتدرا واستشاط الناس في عصريهما بخلاف عم حتى اشتهرا هو الله ومن لم يقل ذاك أحال القدرا أثبتوا رباً ولكن زعموا أنه ممتنع أن يرى وأراد الله إيضاح الهدى حين زاغوا بفتى من أشعرا في صميم النجب الأنصار من خير من يوم حنين نصرا أوضح الحجة حتى ظهرت وأعز الحق حتى استظهرا
The shaykh Abu al-Husayn ibn al-Mubarak ibn Muhammad al-Baghdadi, known as Ibn al-Khall, recited for us in Baghdad in the Nizamiyya school a poem of his in which he praised shaykh Abu al-Futuh Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Muhammad al-Isfarayini, may God have mercy on him, and mentioned from it his saying: 'Disgrace [?] from them of al-Mu'tadid the people, so he was not for the oppressed but a supporter. And he was followed by al-Muktafi bi'llah, who over everything presents al-Muqtadir. And the people became enraged in their two eras due to differing in what they were known for [?] is God, and whoever does not say that attributes fate. They affirmed a Lord but claimed that He is impossible to see. And God wanted to clarify guidance when they deviated, through a young man from Ash'ari, from the core of the noble Ansar, from the best of those who gave victory on the day of Hunayn. He clarified the proof until it appeared and made the truth mighty until it became victorious.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage records a poem by Ibn al-Khall praising al-Ash'ari, though the poem is nominally in praise of al-Isfarayini. The poem first mentions two Abbasid caliphs, al-Mu'tadid and al-Muktafi, and their support of justice. It then criticizes those who make a claim about God that attributes fate (perhaps denial of divine decree) and notes that they affirmed a Lord but denied that He can be seen. This is a reference to the Mu'tazila who deny the vision of God in the afterlife. The poem says God wanted to clarify guidance through 'a young man from Ash'ari', meaning Abul Hasan al-Ash'ari, who is described as from the Ansar (the helpers, referring to his lineage as a descendant of the Ansar from the tribe of Ash'ari). The poem says he clarified the proof and made truth victorious.

Key Terms
المعتضد
المكتفي
المقتدر
القدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) al-Mu'tadid
B) al-Muktafi
C) al-Qadir
D) al-Muqtadir

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Khall's poem sets the historical stage by highlighting the support of caliphs and the people's confusion about divine decree before al-Ash'ari's clarification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you truly believe that everything is under God's control, or do you feel as though fate is random? How does this poem's portrayal of people's anger and confusion reflect your own struggles with trusting God's decree?
?

?
The poem mentions those who say 'He is God and whoever does not say that has changed fate.' This is a strong statement. Is your belief in God's control over your life so firm that you'd confidently say that? What doubts do you need to confront?
